[\[4\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventosar%2Cventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventosar%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>]
And it is necessary that on the day of cupping therapy and bloodletting, the patient should drink more than they eat. [\[5\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventozacio%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventozacio%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>]
And it is necessary to give certain people theriac or medicines with musk, such as musk or aloe wood, before cupping therapy or bloodletting, in order to comfort the main members and make the blood more subtle: but it is not necessary for them to take strong laxatives. [\[6\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventozadament%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventozadament%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>] [\[7\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventozar%2Cventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventozar%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>]
But cupping glasses that are used without scarification should be placed on the liver, above the spleen and gout, above the navel, on the joints of the hands and feet, and on the bladder in the loins: because these members cannot tolerate scarification.
And cupping glasses are not used for those members except to draw blood from the limb, such as placing a cupping glass on the breast in cases of nosebleed; and they use this method to dissolve cold and thick windiness in the member, such as placing it on the abdomen and navel: because it rarefies the member and warms it up, and removes pain by resolving wind.[\[8\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>]
And cupping glasses can be placed on the lungs when there is thick phlegm or mucus in them: because due to strong attractive force, they easily draw out phlegm and remove it from its place.[\[9\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventosacio%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventosacio%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>]
And similarly, it works when the cupping glass is placed on the liver and spleen in cases of windiness trapped inside them.[\[10\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventosar%2Cventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventosar%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>] [\[11\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventozar%2Cventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventozar%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>][\[12\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventosar%2Cventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventosar%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>][\[13\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventosar%2Cventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventosar%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>][\[14\]: <https://books.google.com/ngrams/graph?content=ventozar%2Cventosa%2Cventosacion%2Cventosatorio&year_start=1500&year_end=2008&corpus=23&smoothing=3&share=&direct_url=t1%3B%2Cventozar%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosa%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosacion%3B%2Cc0%3B.t1%3B%2Cventosatorio%3B%2Cc0>]
And these cupping glasses are to be applied only with suction; and sometimes they are applied with fire.
And sometimes they are applied fully filled with warm water in cases of diseases of the spleen.
And that is why the cupping glass should be filled and made larger, which is done with warm water alone or water in which suitable herbs have been boiled.
Method of applying a cupping glass with fire: the width of the orifice should be two fingers, as we have made it; and the quantity of the pressure should be the size of a palm; and it should be in the middle of it, in the place that is on the circuit, the same size as it, the opening should be small according to the quantity that enters it, the size of a needle.
It should be made of iron or citrus wood, with wide and thick edges, like a lion, smooth and polished so that it does not hurt the member when placed on it.
And in the middle of it there should be a transverse bar of iron or of fire, where a candle with fire is placed.
And this cupping glass should be significantly larger than the one we recommended earlier; and smaller according to the illness and the condition of the person using it.
And the cupping glasses for infants and weak individuals are different from those of young and sturdy individuals.
And the way to apply this cupping glass with fire on the member is to extend a wick of decent wood fire in front of a small candle made of wax, and place it in the middle of the transverse bar at the center of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit around its edge, but not burning the patient's skin.
And then it should be placed on the patient's member, and the finger should be placed over the opening that we mentioned, until when we hold the cupping glasses, and do not need to press the finger, but rather let the vapor rise over that opening, then the cupping glass will soon become loose.
And do the same with fire, in its way, and repeat that if necessary.
But for a cupping glass used in the treatment of pleurisy with water, without a transverse bar or opening, it is only filled with water and then placed on the member.
And this is the shape of it: And the larger this cupping glass is, the better it is, so that it can receive a large quantity of water.
Chapter 97.
Leeches are not applied to small members but rather to those where it is impossible to apply cupping glasses due to their small size, such as the mouth and gums or denuded members without flesh, such as the finger and nose and similar areas.
And the way to use those that have been found is to search for leeches in clean, stagnant waters.
And they should be left in stagnant water during the day and night until they are hungry, ensuring that there is no residual cause in their bellies, and evacuate their bowels at the beginning with phlebotomy and cupping glasses; and rub the patient's member until it turns red, then place them on it.
And when they are full and satiated.
And if it is possible to apply suction with a cupping glass in that location, it is more beneficial.
And if not, then wash the area with vinegar and cold water, and rub it and press it.
And if the wound from the leech continues to bleed, and it oozes, then soak a piece of linen in cold water and place it over the wound until the oozing stops.
And if the bleeding is excessive, then sprinkle vitriol trisulphate or other styptic agents on it, until the bleeding stops; or place hot fava beans over the area and leave them there until they stick to the skin, and the bleeding will stop.
And it is necessary, if necessary, to repeat the application of leeches, when possible to replace them.
And if the use of leeches is not possible due to suspension, then rub the area with fresh blood or insert a needle into the location until it bleeds; and then place them, because leeches quickly move towards any source of blood.
And when you want them to fall off, sprinkle a little bit of aloe trisulphate, salt or ashes on them and they will quickly detach themselves.
And so begins the third section of this treatise: on restoration. This chapter is very necessary in the art of medicine, and it deals with the restoration of bone fractures and dislocations caused by accidents.
My son, know that the ignorant doctors and the uneducated laypeople boast of this chapter, those who never studied the books of the Ancients or read a single letter from them.
And this is why the science of the ancients has been lost in our land, and I have not forgotten it at any time; rather, I have only used what I learned later through long study and analysis of the works of the ancients, extracting their knowledge and making it my own.
And I continued to conduct experiments and engage in study throughout my life.
I have described in this chapter all that my science contains and on which I conducted experiments; for I have condensed it here for your convenience and delivered it from the distraction of lengthy exposition; and I have summarized it in a brief summary at the end, and demonstrated it clearly at the end of the explanation; and I have shown many forms of the instruments used in this practice, as an addition to the explanation, just as I did in two preceding chapters.
Chapter one.
Summary and collections on bone fractures, from which a preliminary discussion is necessary before we begin to recall the bones of each member and their various types. Therefore, it is necessary to provide in the introduction of this chapter an overview of the subject matter, and the chapters that form the foundation upon which the author relies, and on which the reader must focus in order to understand and master this noble art, as well as teach it to others.
Therefore, I say that when a fracture or dislocation or sprain or wound occurs, it is necessary to begin with phlebotomy or cauterization of the affected area, or both, if there is no prohibition due to factors such as debility, age (either infancy or old age), or extreme heat or cold.
And the diet should consist of fresh herbs and bird meat and crabs; and it is forbidden to eat heavy meats or foods that fill the veins with blood, until the danger of abscess has passed, and if there is no fear of effusion of matter in the affected member.
And then the patient should return to their usual regimen and lifestyle that they were accustomed to before.
Therefore, when the broken bone begins to heal, it is necessary for the patient to eat nutritious foods that are rich and heavy, with a high viscosity, such as wheat bread baked with water, rice, peas, beef, poultry, fresh fish, thick wine, and similar foods. With this regimen, the healing of the fracture will occur more easily and smoothly.
And know that bones that are fractured in those who are very young or elderly will not heal and consolidate as they naturally should due to the dryness of their members.
However, nature has provided a mechanism for the healing of bones that involves gluing together any similar causes, such as thickened matter that adheres and strengthens the bone until it becomes consolidated and united, like fire at the beginning, until some factor prevents it from performing its function.
And for this reason, it is necessary to refrain from giving the patient food that is heavy, viscous, and large in quantity, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are different according to the different parts of the body, for a fracture of the bone of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head; and a fracture of the bone of the arm is different from a fracture of the bone of the back; and similarly, all other members are different from one another.
I will now recall for you all types of fractures, explained in their own separate chapter and distinguished from the others.
There is a great variety of fractures in themselves. And the types of fractures in bones themselves are varied; for some are curved fractures, others are star-shaped fractures; and there are certain types of fractures based on the length of the bone: and these are the fractures of splinters and shards and protruding and non-protruding additions, as is done in a wound when it is prepared to be written on, and is a fracture with contusion and disruption at the head, and is a small fissure fracture.
And each type has its own specific treatment and remedy in restoring it, according to what will come to mind as a separate recollection in its own places.
There are signs of bone fractures. [*Note: The word "senhal" is similar to the French word "signal", meaning sign or indication.] And this is how a bone fracture is recognized: it is by the deformity and swelling of the affected area, as well as the sensation and appearance upon touch with the hand. Additionally, there is discomfort or pain when pressure is applied to the affected area with the hand.
And therefore, if there is no visible deformity or swelling in the area and when touched there is no abnormal sensation, as well as the patient experiencing little pain upon pressure being applied to the bone, then it is not a fracture.
It is possible that there may be a deformity or small fissure, and therefore you should not manipulate the area with your hand or apply compression. Instead, pause over the area with one of the medicines that comes to mind at that moment that suits the location; and let it be applied with gentle traction rather than forceful compression.
And know that when the affected area is curved and not split in two or showing signs of dislocation, such as swelling throughout its entire length, then it is necessary to begin at once with correcting the curvature, but only if there are signs of abscess or pus formation. [*Note: The word "apostema" is similar to the Latin word "abscessus", meaning a collection of pus.]
And if an abscess forms, then leave it alone for several days until the abscess dissipates; and apply remedies to it in any way that you can, using lightness and skill.
And know that restoration and healing of the affected area is easier than the healing of a bone fracture, which has become dislocated.
And apply strength to it according to what comes to mind in your recollection of appropriate treatments.
But if the bone has become dislocated, then it is necessary to extend the broken member in two parts, whether it be an arm or a leg; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes or one rope and your hand.
And your position should be such that you keep the limb in its natural shape as it is extended, allowing for the broken parts to return to their proper places with skill and in any way possible.[*Note: The phrase "en gen" can also be translated as "with care", depending on the context.]
And take care not to cause further pain or injury to the patient through your operation, and strive to apply the bone to its companion in the best possible way, according to your abilities.
And it is necessary that you manipulate the affected area with your hand at that time, and if you notice any other reason for concern, correct it and treat it to the best of your abilities.
And avoid violent extension and compression, as foolish doctors often cause abscesses or muscle contractions through their operations, which we have seen many times before in the work of such doctors.[*Note: The phrase "metges folls" can also be translated as "reckless surgeons", depending on the context.]
And the quality of support for the limb should be as follows: you must know that broken limbs are varied in their smallness and largeness and shape. [*Note: The word "strictura" can also be translated as "support", referring to the use of bandages or other medical devices to immobilize and align the broken bone during healing. The phrase "parvitat e en lor grandeza" can also be translated as "size and shape", depending on the context.]
And for those that are small, such as arms and fingers, it is necessary to use a wrapping of fine fabric. [*Note: The word "focil" can also be translated as "thumb", depending on the context.]
But for those that are large, such as the thigh or leg, it is necessary to use a thick and firm wrapping, for the toughness of the fabric connects the large and strong member in all parts with even support, preventing any debris from entering the wound.
And begin by applying a wrapping of equalization and traction to the affected area in three or four layers, according to the size of the limb. Then apply gentle pressure with your hand on the bandages.[*Note: The word "evacuacio" can also be translated as "wrapping", referring to the use of bandages or other medical devices to immobilize and align the broken bone during healing. The phrase "pausar liament convenient ad aquo" can also be translated as "apply traction evenly", depending on the context.] [*Note: The word "liament" can also be translated as "ligament", depending on the context. However, in this case it is more likely referring to a medical device used for traction and immobilization.
And then come to the uppermost part of the fracture site and apply less tension than the pressure used to strengthen the affected area. [*Note: The phrase "la partida pus sobirana" can also be translated as "the higher end", depending on the context.] And then gradually extend the bandaging over the fracture site and slowly lessen the tension, until you reach the desired length of the limb.
And then take another bandage and wrap it around the other side of the fracture site with bandaging, gradually moving towards the lower end of the fracture. Your operation should involve applying even tension to the bandages and slowly lessening the pressure as you go.[*Note: The phrase "la partida pus dejos" can also be translated as "the lower end", depending on the context.]
And then place between the layers of bandaging a piece of lamb's wool or fabric that is evenly cut to straighten the curvature of the fracture, if there is any. If not, do not add anything between the layers.[*Note: The phrase "volucios de stopa leon" can also be translated as "layers of sheepskin", depending on the context.] [*Note: The word "am" can be used in both Latin and French to mean "with". In this case, it is being used in a similar way to the French preposition "avec", meaning "with" or "using".][*Note: The phrase "engualada la tortuositat" can also be translated as "correcting the deformity", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "sino" can be used in both Latin and French to mean "if not", depending on the context.]
And then place another bandage over it, and apply stars evenly to these layers of bandaging that have been properly made, but only if there is no swelling or abscess present in the area. If there is an abscess or swelling, then leave it alone for several days before applying the stars. Then strengthen the stars with your hands.[*Note: The phrase "stelhas decentment faytas" can also be translated as "evenly made stars", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "apostema" can be used in both Latin and French to mean "abscess", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "imflacio" can also be translated as "swelling", depending on the context.][*Note: The phrase "layssa aquela per alcus jorns" can also be translated as "leave it alone for several days", depending on the context.][*Note: The phrase "strenh sobre lu las stelhas" can also be translated as "strengthen the stars with your hands", depending on the context.
And let the stars be made of cane with thin strips or from the wood of pine trees or from palm leaves, and let each star be formed wisely in this manner and shape:[*Note: The phrase "be formada saviament" can also be translated as "wisely formed", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "canna" can also be translated as "reed" or "bamboo", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "fust" can also be translated as "stick" or "branch", depending on the context.][*Note: The phrase "ample de quascuna stelha" can also be translated as "the width of each star", depending on the context.
However, it is necessary that the star placed over the fracture be larger and wider than the other stars by a small degree, and the length of the star should be according to the size of the limb. Then strengthen the stars with another bandage according to your first level of tension, and then tie them in place with a string or cord that is evenly formed as we have said regarding tension, which should be according to the location of the fracture and decrease as it moves further away from the fracture site. [*Note: The phrase "lia desus am fiel" can also be translated as "tie them in place with a string or cord", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "fiel" can also be translated as "ligament", depending on the context. However, in this case it is more likely referring to a medical device used for traction and immobilization. [*Note: The phrase "es que sia la tua strictura segon le loc de la fractura mes, e quant mes es alonguat de la fractura, es mendre la strictura" can also be translated as "and let the tension correspond to the location of the fracture, and decrease as it moves further away from the fracture site", depending on the context.
And it is necessary that the strings be medium in thickness and fine, and made of linen; and if the strings are thick, as I have seen some foolish doctors use cords made of twisted fibers, this is a great error, for it can lead to uneven tension and hinder the healing process. [*Note: The phrase "fols pausantz les lors fils de cordas de li tort" can also be translated as "reckless doctors using twisted linen cords", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "li" is an old French word for "linen".[*Note: The word "corda" can also be translated as "rope", depending on the context. However, in this case it is more likely referring to a medical device used for traction and immobilization.
And also fine strings are not suitable, for they will not provide the tension that is desired, and it is not necessary for there to be a space of more than one finger's width between each star. [*Note: The phrase "subtils no so convenientz" can also be translated as "fine ones are not appropriate", depending on the context.][*Note: The phrase "entre astela e astela haja" can also be translated as "between each star there is", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "dita" can also be translated as "fingers", depending on the context.
And if the patient is harmed by the ends of the stars near the tightening at their locations, then place a piece of wool or red wool under them to prevent injury.[*Note: The phrase "de leon" can also be translated as "of lion", depending on the context. However, in this case it is more likely referring to lamb's wool or sheepskin.][*Note: The word "carmenada" can also be translated as "crimson", depending on the context. In this case, it is likely referring to a type of red wool that was commonly used for medical purposes in medieval times.
Sometimes a fracture occurs with contusion, leading to disruption of the tendon. In such cases, I will provide specific instructions in a separate chapter according to the unique nature of the injury.[*Note: The word "plagua" can also be translated as "blow", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "disrupcio" can also be translated as "rupture", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "codena" can also be translated as "tendon", depending on the context. [*Note: The phrase "segon singularitat" can also be translated as "according to its unique characteristics", depending on the context.
And know that not all broken limbs can be immobilized with stars on the first day; for if the limb is large, it is not necessary to apply the stars immediately, but rather wait a few days or a week or more, depending on your confidence in preventing abscess formation. [*Note: The phrase "aprop v dias o vii" can also be translated as "a few days or a week", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "seguratat" can also be translated as "confidence", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "adveniment" can also be translated as "occurrence", depending on the context.
And this is the method of applying plasters, which ancient doctors used to restore bones that were broken, dislocated, or twisted.
Here is a method for applying a plaster that can be used to treat fractures, and it is particularly suitable for many complex cases, especially those involving women and children, as it does not produce heat or friction. To make this plaster, take some powdered flour from the grinding stone in the mill, which is made from the inner part of the grain after it has been ground between the walls of the mill.
And sieve the flour through egg white, and apply the mixture in a thick but not too fine layer, and then use it as needed.[*Note: The word "comfeys" can also be translated as "prepare", depending on the context.][*Note: The phrase "cribelles aquel am album de huou" can also be translated as "sift the flour with egg white", depending on the context.][*Note: The word "album" can also be translated as "egg white", depending on the context.
Another way of making an emplaster to heal fractures and dislocations and twists: take assafetida, lapdanum, acacia, enula, migrach (which is wild marjoram), all in the amount of one drachma; myrrh and aloe each in the amount of five drachmas; aloe in the amount of twenty drachmas; grind them and sift them and mix them with almond oil or egg white if the patient's complexion is dry. Then use this emplaster, for it has a good composition and heals lightly fractured bones and benefits many people.
Another way of making an emplaster to heal fractures and twists: take migrach, white mallow each in the amount of one drachma; myrrh and aloe each in the amount of five drachmas; acacia six drachmas; Armenian bole twenty drachmas.
Grind all the ingredients together in a mortar with a pestle, then sift them and mix with water or egg white, and then administer it (the plaster) as needed.
Another way of making a plaster for dislocated joints and bones out of place, to alleviate pain in those areas and heal cracks and fractures: take raw wool, soak it in vinegar and heated oil, then apply it to the affected area.
But this plaster does not have the power of healing or drawing out abscesses and properly expelling pains.
Another way of making a plaster that heals broken bones: take leaves from the wild fig tree and poppy plant, grind them all together and make a plaster out of it.
Another way of making a quick-acting plaster for healing bone fractures and promoting resolution of the remaining abscess: take equal parts of mallow root, chamomile, violet flowers, and barley flour, all ground together in equal proportions. Grind all the ingredients together and mix with wine, if the area is not scaly or inflamed.
But if it is scaly or inflamed, then soothe it with coltsfoot water or plain water and use it as needed.
Another way of making a stronger plaster for abscess healing than the previous one, when you need to heal an abscess and restore the affected limb: take equal parts of mallow root, flax seeds, balmony, melilot, marjoram, violet flowers, chamomile, all ground together and combined with willow water or plain water or wine, depending on the quality of the limb and its color.
However, Hippocrates says in his book that a plaster should not be placed on the broken limb for its healing, but rather a cerate made of wax and oil, with no other ingredients; and he advises that it should be of medium consistency, neither too thick nor too thin.
But in Galen's work, it is seen that plasters can be placed on the broken limb for its healing and they use dry substances with a little heat, such as myrrh, cautery instruments, and similar items to those mentioned before.
Another way of making a plaster that helps with injury and pain: take half as much of the following ingredients: goat dung, human shaved hair or bird feathers, mallow root, and salt, all ground and sifted together and made into a plaster.
But the amount of time that the plaster should remain applied and then be removed depends on whether pain or swelling occurs, or if the bone is out of place; in such cases, you should not remove it for many days. And if pain or swelling occurs at the affected area, then you should consider removing the plaster immediately and applying a new one.
And take a piece of linen and a wet sponge, and soak them in warm water, then use those to gently wash the affected area until the swelling subsides and the pain is alleviated; then let the limb rest for an hour; and afterwards place on it a wool compress soaked in vinegar and oil or rose oil, and leave it on for a night, until you are certain that pus has been drawn out, and the swelling of the limb has decreased, and the pain has subsided.
And then repeat the gentle pressure and light application of the plaster, gradually increasing the strength as the injury heals.
And if you see that the abscess and inflammation and pain and all other symptoms have completely subsided, and there is no longer a need for plasters or pressure, then repeat the treatment as before.
And if none of the symptoms mentioned occur in the limb, then you should remove the plaster after three or four or five or seven days. And sometimes everything is left in place for 20 days, as you see fit based on the removal of the plaster, until the bone heals and the flesh begins to form over it.
Then increase the pressure gradually, rather than immediately as before, and adjust it based on the patient's nutritional status and overall health condition.
But if you see that the site of the fracture is drying out and becoming weakened more than necessary, then you should know that nutrition is not able to reach it; and so, immerse it in warm water every time you change the dressing, for three days; and slightly decrease the pressure, and through this procedure allow the limb to receive nourishment, and thus heal quickly.
But sometimes foolish healers of bone fractures, when the healing does not begin properly, try to restore it in a twisted or crooked way, which is a mistake according to proper medical practice and causes great fear; if they were correct, then ancient physicians would have recorded this method in their books.
And I have never seen such a thing done by any of them, so it is more reasonable not to do it.
Chapter Two: On Fractures in the Head.
Chapter Two: On Fractures of the Head
And the extent of these two types of fractures can be large or small, and may involve crushing or scraping; the cause of such injuries is typically blunt force trauma from falling onto rocks or other hard surfaces.
This type of fracture may be penetrating, reaching the periosteum (the layer of tissue covering the bone), or it may occur on the surface of the skull bone; and the extent of these two types of fractures can be large or small.
Another type of fracture is one that occurs in the thin layer of bone beneath the hair, and for this reason, it is called "capillary" fracture.
Another type of fracture is one caused by impact or force, which causes a thin layer of bone to be pushed into the interior of the skull, similar to what happens when tiles are struck and break apart; this type of fracture occurs in concave areas, such as those found in the parietal bones.
This type of fracture is most commonly found in the skulls of infants or in any areas where the bone is more flexible and pliable, such as the forehead.[ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)
And all these types of fractures are classified as "satagias," which means they have separate fragments or pieces that have become detached from one another. I will provide a summary and review of all the topics discussed in their proper places.
And these types of fractures can be identified by examination and investigation, including probing for loose fragments and examining for signs of infected or necrotic tissue surrounding the wound.
A capillary fracture can be identified by the appearance of discoloration or bruising on the surface of the skull bone, and when probed with a blunt instrument, it will feel like a crack rather than a break through the entire thickness of the bone.[ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)
When treating a fracture, it is important to first consider the overall health and condition of the patient, and if there are any signs or symptoms that suggest a serious complication, such as vomiting due to cholera, extensive swelling, loss of consciousness, change in voice, fainting, high fever, swollen hands or feet, severe pain, or other concerning symptoms. If present, treatment should be delayed until the patient's condition has stabilized and there is no further risk to their health.[ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)
If there are no concerning symptoms or complications, then treatment can begin.
The reason for this is that if the injury occurs in the winter and the wound appears severe, it is important to wait at least 14 days before beginning treatment, as this will allow any swelling or inflammation to subside, making it easier to accurately assess the extent of the damage and plan an effective course of treatment. [ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)
However, if the injury occurs during the summer months, it is important to begin treatment within 7 days, as any damage to the tissue beneath the skull may begin to decompose and become infected, leading to serious complications and health risks.
If the fracture has reached the layer of tissue covering the brain and is made up of small fragments or has become crushed, then it is necessary to surgically remove any loose pieces of bone, as recommended by me.[ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)
When treating a fractured skull, it is important to first carefully examine the wound and remove any damaged tissue or debris that may be present on the surface of the bone. This can be done using standard surgical techniques, taking care to avoid causing further injury or damage to the surrounding tissues. Once the wound has been cleaned and prepared for treatment, the next step is to assess the extent of the fracture and develop a plan for repair. [ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/) [ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)
If during the examination of the wound, you discover signs of bone damage, such as swelling or pus, it is important to take action to address these issues before proceeding with treatment. This can be done by applying warm compresses soaked in vinegar and rose oil to the affected area, allowing the swelling and inflammation to subside, and ensuring that any bleeding has stopped. Once this has been accomplished, you can begin the surgical repair of the fracture, carefully removing damaged tissue and realigning the bones as needed. [ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/) [ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)[ ](https://hippomedicina.com/blog/item/la-medicina-en-la-edat-media/)
There are two main approaches to surgical treatment of skull fractures: open reduction and internal fixation (ORIF), or closed reduction and external fixation (CREF). In ORIF, the surgeon makes an incision in the scalp, exposing the fracture site and allowing direct visualization of the damage. The bones are then realigned and held in place with screws, plates, or other hardware. In CREF, the surgeon does not make an incision, but instead uses external fixation devices to stabilize the bones from outside the body. Both methods have their advantages and disadvantages, and the choice of approach depends on the nature and severity of the injury, as well as the patient's individual needs and circumstances.
One surgical approach is to use a narrow-bladed chisel or osteotome to carefully cut through the bone, creating an incision along the fracture line. This can help to loosen and remove any damaged tissue or debris that may be obstructing the wound. The surgeon then uses a second, wider blade to further expand the incision and gain access to the deeper layers of the skull.
And then apply the incision cut wider on the first [incision] than on the second.
It is necessary that there are various kinds of incisors, some of which are wider than others; and some are shorter than others and should be at the back with the sharpest points, and they should be made of iron or steel.
And use them with ease in percussion on the incisors, so that it does not cause harm or damage to the head (brain).
And if the bone is strong and hard, then it is necessary to drill into its surface before making incisions with the trepan (an instrument for boring holes in the skull), which are called non-penetrating trephines.
And they are not called that (non-penetrating trephines) because they do not reach the bone of the skull, but rather it is because the end of the trepan is round or has a sharp edge like a small circle, which prevents it from being submerged and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is appropriate to have a large number of these trephines, each one suitable for the thickness of each bone, so that the sharp end of the trepan is present in all parts of the skull with the length and width according to the thickness of the skull.
And this is the method for three types of trephines: The quality of drilling into the surface of a fractured bone is that you place the trepan on the bone and rotate it with your hands until you know that the bone has been penetrated; then move the trepan to another location, placing what is between all the holes the distance of the thickness of the drill or around that.
And then make incisions with the incisors into what is between each hole in the bone, making it as fine as possible to remove the bone fragments, either with your hand or with some other tool that you have prepared for this purpose, such as probes and fine forceps.
And it is necessary to be careful that the incisors do not touch any part of the brain.
And once you have drilled into the bone and separated it from the brain if they are connected, and extracted it, it is necessary to rasp and grind down the roughness on the surface of the bone that remains in the skull with another tool similar to an incisor, but it should be sharper and thinner than the other incisors.
And if there remains any small and stuck fragments of bone, then remove them easily with the appropriate tool, and then take care of the wound with liniments and ointments as we have said before.
And the other method of operation is light and safe, praised by Galen, and this is its description: it is necessary for you at the beginning to reveal the part of the bone in the location where the fracture is most intense and obvious. Once you have revealed that part and placed the blunt end of this incisor on top of it, which has this shape:
And it is that the lens-shaped part is blunt and does not cut into anything, and the pointed part is located two finger widths in front, as you can see; and the lens-shaped part should be against the brain with the sharp face of the incisor cutting into the bone. [Translation note: The word "lenticular" means lens-shaped, and here it refers to the rounded end of the tool.]
And then strike the incisor with a small mallet in one blow until you have cut through the bone easily, as it rotates, and make sure that the fatty tissue does not come into contact with the instrument in any way, and if the physician is the most foolish and ignorant of all men and even if he is asleep.
And if there remains any fatty tissue attached to the bone in some places, then cut it away from the bone with the lens-shaped end of the incisor easily and carefully, as it is now free from harm and danger.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate into the fatty tissue, and what has been cut away is only the surface of the bone, leaving roughness and fine splinters behind, then rasp the roughness and remove those splinters with fine rasps that you have prepared in various sizes so that you can use the most appropriate and correct ones according to the location and shape of the broken bone.
And it is necessary to start rasping the bone with the widest rasp, and then gradually use finer ones until you reach the finest of them all. Do not stop doing this in a continuous motion until you have completed the operation using the sharpest and finest rasps.
And in other small or subtle fractures, you should administer care according to what is necessary for the correction of the injury.
And it is important to note that not all injuries are alike, and each one requires individual study and attention. And now we have discussed what has been written about major fractures.
And if the fatty tissue is retracted when you remove the bone, then take a piece of it according to the size of the wound, soak it in oil and place it over the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of cloth, double or triple-layered, soak it in wine and oil, and place it over the first piece. Do this as gently as possible so that you do not damage the brain. And then apply a wide bandage, but do not pull it tight, only enough to cover the wound.
And leave it with this care for one or two days until you are sure that there is no danger of abscess, and then remove the bandages and administer medicines to reduce excess dryness, such as linseed rinses, powdered iris root, and plantain and arnica powders, among others. [Translation note: Aristologia is a type of herb or plant that was used in medieval medicine.]
And make a powder from these medicines and sprinkle it on the wound while it is dry. And generally, use any medicine that has the property of drying without causing pain or irritation.
And make sure to keep the wound clean and free from any infection or other contamination while you treat it. Do not allow any pus to accumulate in it, as this could lead to corruption of the fatty tissue surrounding the brain and cause serious harm to the patient.
And if there is a blackening or discoloration on the surface of the skull, and the bone appears to be necrotic, especially if care for the wound has been neglected, then you should consider that the patient may suffer from the consequences that we have mentioned. [Translation note: The word "parnicios" is an old form of the verb "perniciare", which means to harm or damage irreparably, often used in a medical context to refer to the spread of infection or necrosis.]
And if the discoloration is caused by the medicine that has been applied to it and not from any infection or other cause, then you should take one part of honey and three parts of oil and mix them together. [Translation note: The use of honey as a healing agent was common in medieval medicine.] [Continued in next message] And soak a piece of cloth in this mixture and place it over the wound. Then care for the wound with appropriate medical treatments until it is healed. [Translation note: The word "linieys" means "anoints", referring to the application of a medicinal substance.]

On the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken unless two pieces are raised up together or one of them has been significantly displaced, because there are two bones in the nose and the lower part is cartilaginous and does not break easily, only becoming bruised, swollen, and misshapen. [Translation note: The word "ditet" means "finger", referring here to the use of a finger or fingers to apply pressure and manipulate the broken bone.] And do this carefully, being mindful not to cause further pain or injury to the patient during the procedure. [Translation note: The word "caja" means "to harm", "injure", or "hurt".]

[Translation note: The word "vii" means "seventh", and "x" means "tenth". This suggests that the recommended waiting period is either one week or ten days.]

Chapter IV
Of the restoration of the jaw when it is dislocated.
When the jawbone is only fractured and the break is not open, look then to see if the fracture is displaced only on the outside, and is not broken into two pieces but rather curved inward. If this is the case, its healing will be easy.
And so, if the fracture is on the right side, you should insert your left thumb into the patient's mouth; and likewise, if the fracture is on the left side of the jawbone, insert your right thumb. Then gently push the swelling of the fracture inward with your other hand from the outside of the bone. Do this slowly and evenly.
And if the jawbone is broken into two separate pieces, then you must apply extension to both parts as much as possible until they can be aligned.
And if there is also dislocation of the teeth due to the fracture, then strengthen those that you expect will remain with wires made of gold or silver or some other strong material. Then place wax over the broken jawbone, followed by a doubled cloth, and then a thick, well-made bandage or a sole pressed into the shape of the jawbone; and bind this in place as you have prepared your bandaging materials, until healing is complete.
Command the patient to rest and be still, and provide them with soft food that can be easily chewed or even pureed.
And if you believe that the bone has healed incorrectly in any way, then re-examine the patient after three days and correct what is misaligned. Apply a plaster of powdered molasses, albumen from eggs, or powdered cumin to the area, once you have removed the original wax. Place wool over the plaster.
And once the plaster is affixed and properly aligned with the bone, leave it in place until the break has healed and the bone has regained its strength, which often takes only three weeks. Do not disturb the plaster during this time, as this could interfere with healing.
And if an abscess forms at the wound site, then administer what we have often said before in treating it with soothing remedies until it has healed and the abscess has receded.
And if the break is open, consider whether there is a piece of bone missing or damaged. If so, be prepared to remove any shattered fragments with appropriate instruments during surgery.
And if the wound is narrow, then widen it using a trepan (a surgical instrument) as much as necessary for your procedure.
And once you have removed any bone fragments and there is no remaining debris, then close the wound if it is large; if not, apply an appropriate plaster to promote healing and help the wound close. Do this until the wound has healed completely.
Chapter V.
Of restoring the fork (furcula) bone when it is dislocated.
Often, the fork (furcula) bone is broken at the front where it connects to the muscle, and this break can occur in one of three ways.
Because it is either broken, separated into two pieces (other than having shattered fragments), or broken in such a way that there are shattered fragments. The first of these is easy to restore, while the second requires more effort, and the third type is even more difficult as it involves an open fracture. [**Note**: I am assuming "furcula" refers to a bone in the body, possibly the clavicle or collarbone, given the context of broken bones. However, "furcula" can also refer to a fork in Old and Middle English, which may be a more appropriate translation depending on the specific anatomy being discussed.]
And the procedure when the break is not open involves two assistants: one holds the injured party's head still and guides the broken clavicle back into place, while the other extends the neck toward the affected area.
Then align the broken ends of the clavicle using your fingers until they are properly formed and do not protrude or curve inward too much. [**Note**: I am assuming "furcula" refers to the clavicle based on the context of broken bones, but it could also refer to a fork as mentioned earlier. Please clarify if my assumption is incorrect.]
If more extension is needed, place a roll of cloth or wool under the patient's neck to provide additional support and lift the clavicle into position. Gently compress the roll with your hand to align the fracture as needed. [**Note**: I am still assuming "furcula" refers to the clavicle based on the context, but please let me know if this is incorrect.]
If you are unable to pull the end of the clavicle outward because it has become lodged against the sternum, then have the patient tilt their head back and place a medium-sized cushion under the affected muscle. This will help to elevate the clavicle so that it can be more easily realigned.
Have one assistant press down on the muscle at the back of the clavicle to lift the bone away from the sternum, allowing you to realign the fracture using your fingers as needed.
But if you feel fragments of the clavicle and they are loose, then it is best to cut into the area and gently extract these fragments with ease.
But if the fragments are trapped within the bone, then you must make an incision in the area using one of your prepared scalpels. You should have placed a retractor under the clavicle earlier to provide access for this instrument, which has the following shape:
And it resembles the bowl of a spoon, with a cavity like this:
But the length of this instrument should be as practical as possible for the operation, with two ends that are similar to those of a spoon or ladle, one end being wide and the other narrow.
And if the wound you have created when extracting the fragment of bone is wide and there is a risk of infection, then you should close the two sides of the wound with stitches to prevent further complications.
And if the wound is small or you are concerned about infection, then pack the wound with cloth and powder according to the size of the incision.
And if an abscess forms, then insert a cloth soaked in vinegar, rose oil, and wine into the wound and cover it.
And make sure that the wooden board does not cause any harm to the broken bone; therefore, you should check on the patient every day. If the bandage becomes too loose or you see that the board has shifted, then adjust it accordingly and tighten the bandage as needed.
Also have the patient sleep on their back with a small cushion under their neck and a thin roll placed under the shoulders to keep them propped up during sleep. This will help lift the assistant muscle and elevate the fracture of the clavicle, or alternatively, secure the arm to the patient's neck. [**Note**: I am assuming "assistant muscle" refers to the sternocleidomastoid muscle, which attaches to the clavicle. However, this is just an educated guess and may not be entirely accurate.]
And do not remove the bandage unless there is pus or inflammation in the area within twelve days.
And replace the plaster if you see that it has become dirty or damaged, and adjust it to maintain proper tension as the bone heals. Leave it in place until the fracture has fully healed and any bleeding has stopped.
And in most cases, the bone should be strong and healed within 28 days, though this may vary depending on the individual.
Chapter VI.
Of the restoration of a shoulder fracture.
The shoulder blade (spatula) is broken along its edge but not separated from the bone, only the edges are affected. [**Note**: I am assuming "spatula" refers to the shoulder blade or scapula based on context, but please let me know if this is incorrect.]
And so when the break is in the middle of the bone and not easily visible, you must determine the nature of the fracture through touch and then carefully plan your approach to reducing it to its natural shape using any means necessary.
Then apply a powder made from molasses and egg whites along with a wool compress to the area, followed by a double layer of bandage. Also place a thin wooden board over the entire shoulder blade or slightly less, depending on its size.
But if the wooden board does not conform to the curve of the shoulder blade in some areas, then use wool to fill in any concave spaces and adjust the position of the board as needed to ensure a proper fit.
Then secure the bandage in place using a long, narrow cloth like those used by the Saracens, making sure it is tight enough to hold everything in place but not so tight that it causes discomfort or displaces the bone.
And check the bandage every day, and if it becomes loose or displaced, re-wrap it and adjust the position of the wooden board as needed to maintain proper alignment.
And have the patient lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade should be healed within 20 to 25 days.
And when this time period has passed, you can safely remove the bandage, knowing that the bone is strong and stable and not at risk of re-fracture or other complications.
And if there is a wound at the site of the broken bone and you notice that the skin has been punctured or torn, then you should clean the area with a sterile instrument and remove any foreign matter or debris. If an abscess forms, treat it as described in the section on clavicle fractures. [**Note**: I am assuming "codena" refers to some sort of skin or tissue covering the wound, but I am not sure what it means exactly. Could you please clarify?]
Chapter VII.
Of the restoration of a foot fracture. [**Note**: I am assuming "pietz" refers to the foot, but please let me know if this is incorrect.]
Sometimes the foot is broken in the middle, which can be difficult to treat, but the ends of the bones are more often crushed or shattered. [**Note**: I am assuming "ciccadas" means "crushed" and "comminuted" means "shattered" based on context, but please let me know if this is incorrect.]
When the foot is broken in the middle, it often tilts inward and causes severe pain, difficulty walking, coughing, rapid breathing, lightheadedness, nausea, and even vomiting blood. The break is usually visible or can be felt by touch.
To restore a broken foot, have the patient lie on their back with a cushion beneath their hips and another under their shoulders. Apply pressure to the muscles surrounding the break and realign the bones using your hands, adjusting them as needed until the natural shape of the foot is restored. [**Note**: I am assuming "spallas" means "shoulders" based on context, but please let me know if this is incorrect.]
Then apply a plaster and wool compress to the area, followed by a thin wooden board made of cedar or cherry wood (or similar) that is light enough to be easily moved but strong enough to provide support. Secure the board in place with bandages, taking care not to cause further injury. Adjust the tension of the bandage as needed and check it frequently to ensure it remains secure. [**Note**: I am assuming "caligi" means "cherry wood" based on context, but please let me know if this is incorrect.]
And if the bandage becomes loose due to corrosion, pressure, pain, or infection, then you should remove it and clean the wound before reapplying the plaster and adjusting the tension as needed. Continue to monitor the wound and adjust the bandage as necessary until it has fully healed.
Chapter 8
Of the restoration of ribs when they are broken.
Know that rib fractures do not occur except in large parts where they meet; and at the ends of their front part, such accidents do not happen unless they are cartilaginous. And recognition of it is not hidden by sensation, but rather sought through questioning.
And the restoration should be such that it heals the fracture with the said methods as far as possible, until it is healed as needed, and then apply a plaster and strengthen the broken bone with a bandage, if necessary.
And if the rib fracture tends towards the inner parts, then the patient experiences violent pain and punctures, similar to punctures that occur in one who is pierced, and this happens because the bone touches the lining; and it causes difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting of bloody phlegm; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients were ingenious in that with clever words.
And of what they said: it is necessary to stop the patient from eating foods that cause bloating and flatulence, so that the stomach inflates and expands, and the fracture is pushed outward.
But we avoid that so as not to cause even a slight risk of abscess formation, if it does not happen; and if it does happen, then help it in that case.
And some of them said that a blister should be applied above the swelling and sucked with force; and it is similar to logical reasoning, but it is still feared that the swelling may draw excessive fluids to the area due to weakness.
And some of them said: it is necessary that the area be covered with wool soaked in healing oil, and powders should be placed between the ribs until it is full, that is, the bandage when wrapped around according to its roundness; and then cure the patient with a cure for pleurisy with food and medicine.
And if the patient experiences violent constriction which they cannot bear, and the bone touches the lining with puncture pain, then we fear for the patient, in that case it is necessary to make an incision on the area, reveal the broken rib, and place an instrument that preserves the chest cavity over it, the memory of which is mentioned before, and carefully insert it between the bone and extract it.
And then we should suture the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with stitches, and treat it with bandages until it heals.
And if an abscess forms inside, then you should heat and soak powders in healing oil and place it on the area, and treat the patient with what is necessary for internal abscesses.
And raise them up on a pillow under their back, where they sleep, until it heals.
Chapter 9.
Of the restoration of fractures of the vertebrae in the back and neck.
Sometimes it happens that fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and it also affects their cartilage, similarly to the vertebrae in the back. And if you want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider this: if you see that the patient's muscles are weakened, numb, and paralyzed and cannot move or strengthen them, and when you constrict or prick the area with a needle, they do not feel pain, then you should know that it is not healing, and in most cases, it is fatal. [https://www.diffen.com/difference/Latin\_vs\_Spanish](https://www.diffen.com/difference/Latin_vs_Spanish)
And if they move their limbs and feel pain when constricted or pricked, then you should know that the nape of the bone is saved and the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see similar symptoms in the vertebrae of the neck and want to know whether it will heal or not, then observe their feet. [https://www.diffen.com/difference/Latin\_vs\_Spanish](https://www.diffen.com/difference/Latin_vs_Spanish)
And if you see that their feet are weakened, similar to what happens in the limbs, and when they lie on their backs, there is bloating and involuntary bowel movements, and sometimes they are raised up on their backs and want to urinate but cannot, then you should know that it is fatal, and therefore, do not bother with treating them.
But if none of those symptoms are present, then the condition is better and less severe.
And the treatment of this condition is to make an incision in the swelling and place a compress soaked in healing oil or warm poultices over the compressed vertebra, changing it several times a day until the swelling subsides.
And then apply a strengthening and comforting bandage, and wrap it with linen; also instruct the patient to rest and remain calm and avoid sleeping on the affected side until it heals.
And if it is caused by a separation of the cartilage or some other cause, then you must remove the separated part from the spine and suture the two lips of the wound, if it is large; also treat the affected area with healing ointments until it heals.
But if the tip of the coccyx bone is broken, then you must insert your left index finger into the anus and carefully align the broken bone with the other hand as much as possible; also place a bandage and splint over it, if necessary, and wrap it with a bandage to strengthen it.
And if you feel cartilage fragments in the area, then remove them and treat the wound with the prescribed treatment until it heals.
Chapter 10.
Of the healing of the ankle.
The bones of the ankles are often broken, and when they break, the fracture is not just a clean break but rather a communication at the ends of the bone that extends along its length and tends to bend inward. This causes pain and punctures in the affected area, and the leg becomes weak and numb due to the injury.
The healing process for an ankle fracture involves extending your hand over the affected area, aligning it with the shape of the fracture. This may help to realign and stabilize the broken bones, allowing them to heal properly.
If the fracture only affects the tip of the ankle bone, then you should align it with the rest of the bone according to its natural shape and position. This may involve realigning the bone fragments and holding them in place while they heal.
If the fracture is in the shaft of the bone or is angulated towards the back, then you should have the patient lie on their stomach while you prepare for the treatment of the fracture. This may involve realigning the bones and holding them in place with a cast or splint while they heal. Once you have aligned the bones, apply a bandage or dressing over the area and place a splint or stick on top to hold the bones in place while they heal. Be careful not to apply too much pressure or cause further injury to the affected area.
Once the bones are aligned, fill in the gap with appropriate materials to hold them in place and ensure they heal correctly. In modern medical practice, this may involve using a cast or splint to immobilize the bone fragments while they heal. The patient should be instructed to rest and avoid putting weight on the affected limb until it has fully healed.
If an abscess forms in the affected area, it is important to wait until it has subsided before attempting to realign or heal the fracture. Apply appropriate treatment for the abscess and monitor its progress before resuming treatment for the fracture. Once the abscess has healed, you can resume the healing process for the ankle using the methods described earlier in this text.
If the cartilage in the ankle is damaged or shattered, then you should treat it accordingly. The text does not provide specific instructions for treating damaged cartilage, but it suggests that the practitioner should use their judgment and knowledge to address the issue. In modern medical practice, treatment for damaged cartilage may involve surgery, physical therapy, or other interventions depending on the severity of the injury.
Chapter 11.
This passage discusses the healing process for a fractured adductor muscle, which is also known as the "adjutori" in medieval medical texts. The adductor muscles are located in the thigh and help to move the leg toward the body's midline. Fractures of these muscles can be painful and may require extensive treatment and rehabilitation.
The adjutorii are the muscles located between the hip joint and the head of the femur (thigh bone). These muscles help to flex, extend, and rotate the thigh, and are essential for proper movement and stability of the lower body. Fractures or injuries to these muscles can be painful and debilitating, requiring extensive treatment and rehabilitation.
If the adjutorii are broken, then the healing process for this injury can be done in one of two ways. The first way is to take a flat stick made of medium-sized wood and shape it as follows:
And the two ends of the ligaments should be tied with two strips of linen, and then it should be suspended from an elevated location.
And place the patient on a cushion; and then straighten out their broken arm onto the stick, so that it reaches the armpit, with the curve of the stick attached to the armpit.
And then suspend the patient with some severe cause, or the nurse shall stretch it to the lower parts; and then the doctor shall manipulate the fracture with their hands, until the fracture returns to its proper place.
And the other method is for the patient to be raised by their neck and suspended with a strap; and then order two assistants, one of whom holds what is above the fracture with their hands; and the other holds below; and each of them stretches on their respective side.
And if you want the extension to be stronger, then you (the doctor) should apply traction on the fracture with a strap and also on it another strap, and each of the two assistants stretches on both sides.
And so if the fracture is close to the end of the muscle, then it is appropriate for half of the strap to be placed on the armpit and the other half below the fracture near the knee.
And similarly, if the fracture is close to the knee, then it is appropriate for the strap to be placed on that location and on the knee itself.
Apply that (the strap) with ease and without difficulty, and when it is enough to be applied, then traction should be applied according to what is appropriate and it is applied well, then apply strong traction on it unless a pustule forms as a result of the cautery.
But if a pustule forms as a result of the cautery, then leave the traction for three days and place wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil on it until the pustule heals, and then apply traction again.
The method of strengthening the traction is to place a poultice on the fracture and then wrap it with a bandage, and then attach the arm to the splint, and keep the hand open on the patient's own muscle.
And wrap (the bandage) and apply traction on the splint so that the arm is in a straight position, unless there is any reason forbidding it due to the broken bone.
And then use a stretcher.
And the splint that you use on the fracture should be wider and stronger than the other splints; and there should be a space the width of a finger between each splint, and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers on all sides.
And then apply traction on the splints with the tension that I mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And the tension over the site of the fracture should be tighter. And the longer is the fracture, the less should be the tension.
And so, if you see the placement of the splints and the tension as we have said at the time when you restore the limb to its original position, then do it.
And if you are afraid of a pustule from the cautery, then leave the tension and splints for three days as we have said.
And then consider the strap in all parts for three days to prevent a pustule or inflammation from occurring in the affected area; or if the food is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive tension, then correct it as we have said.
But if you are confident that there will be no problems with leaving it, then do not loosen the bandage but leave it for three days or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on their back, and their hands on their stomach.
And place a pillow filled with soft wool under the splint, and at all times during the night or day check that the shape of the broken limb is not altered or loose, or if the bandage has become too soft, then correct it as much as you can.
And give the patient food according to the order we mentioned before, so that in the beginning the food should be light until traction has begun, and then the food should be heavy (nutritious).
Because it is customary for the splint and its position to be maintained for 40 days, and then they should be loosened and a bath should be administered and it should be treated with ointments that will correct any issues.
And if the fracture is slow to heal, then you should not loosen the bandage and splint for 30 days or for two months.
Chapter 12
On the restoration of a fractured arm
An arm is composed of two bones that are called the two forearm bones; one of which is small and it is called the radius, which lies next to the thumb side, and the other one is large and it is called the ulna, which is placed below the small bone on the side further away from the thumb.
And it is common for only one of the two bones to be fractured, either the larger bone or the smaller one, and it is also common for both bones to be fractured together.
And when the small bone is fractured at the top (proximal end), then the healing process for it is easier and quicker.
And when the larger bone is fractured at the bottom (distal end), then the fracture is severe and the healing process for it is difficult.
And even worse than that is when both bones are fractured together.
And so if the bone that is fractured is the small bone at the top (proximal end), then it is appropriate for the doctor to apply gentle traction with ease until the arm is in a position to be set.
But if it is the large bone that is fractured, then strong traction should be applied.
And if both pupils are crossed, then you should place the instrument very firmly.
And it is necessary to place the impression of the hand below the treatment and extension of it, stretched out over a cushion, with the pulse of the hand at the patient's head, and the ear lower than the other parts, and the patient should be lying on a slanted pillow.
And there should be a cushion in front of the patient at their height, so that the patient does not have to exert themselves.
And the assistant should extend the arm of the affected side lower with their hand or linen, and another assistant should extend it in a similar way above.
And the doctor should manipulate the bone so that it returns to its best shape.
And if there is a fracture in the bone, then you should make an incision and realign the entire bone in its place with your skill.
And if there is a separate fracture that you cannot reduce, then you should perform surgery on it and remove the bone in the manner that we have said above.
But if the fracture is accompanied by an abscess, I have already discussed this in a separate chapter, so follow the treatment for that.
And if an abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then you should make a compress of linen coated in a mixture of roasted oil and white wax, with a balance between softness and fineness, and place it over the abscess with gentle pressure until it subsides. Then remove the compress and apply a plaster prepared from powdered chalk and egg white, and place a metal plate over it. The plate should be placed as close to the fracture as possible, and should be as thin and lightweight as possible.
And know that the number of metal plates used for a broken arm is usually two in the most common configurations, such as when there is a single fracture or a double fracture.
And place your bandage over the site of the fracture with greater force and pressure, and when it is necessary to apply more tension to the tissue above or below the fracture, do so with a lighter touch as described at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be damp and not too tight or rough, and the thread used to sew them together should be of a medium thickness, neither too fine nor too coarse, as we have recommended.
And consider the member and ligaments for several days. And if any cause arises that requires correction, such as swelling in the affected limb, then you should immerse the member in warm water until the swelling subsides.
And leave the member unconstrained until it has rested, and then reapply the bandage.
And if the bandages have softened and the bone has healed and returned to its normal shape, then you should correct any remaining issues with your skill.
And consider whether the flow of nutrients to the limb is being obstructed by excessive tightness in the bandages, in which case you should loosen them slightly and leave them in place for several days until the flow of nutrients has been restored, and then strengthen the area.
But if the patient does not experience any of the complications that we have mentioned, then it is not necessary to remove the bandages for at least 20 days or thereabouts.
And the patient's arm should be suspended using their own strength and mobility, and they should sleep on their back.
And know that this fracture is typically healed in 30 to 32 days, or it may heal more quickly in as few as 28 days, depending on the patient's overall health and the severity of the injury.
Chapter 13.
Chapter 13: On the healing of fractures in the fingers and toes.
Know that fingers and toe nails are particularly susceptible to fractures, but they can also become damaged through repeated friction or wear.
And if there is a fracture or sprain in the hand, then it is necessary for the patient to lie down with their arm extended and centered in front of them, and place their hand on top of it.
And the assistant should extend the broken bones while the doctor manipulates them so that they are realigned properly.
And then you should apply a plaster and bandage, unless there is an abscess present; and in that case, place a metal plate over it according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped that in cloth.
But if the sprain is in the inner parts of the palm, then make a kind of ball from cloth and tell the patient to clench it with their injured hand. It should be strengthened with long strips of cloth.
And let the metal plate be covered with cloth that is lightweight, so that the metal can be applied to the palm; and strengthen it as needed.
But if the fracture is on the outside of the hand, then you should place a metal plate on top and another one on the underside of the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and then apply a bandage, as if the hand is folded at the wrist with linen.
And if there is a fracture in one of the nails of the fingers, then if it is the big toe, it should be manipulated as needed and strengthened with the palm; and if you wish, place a small metal plate on it to elevate the fracture and prevent damage.
But if the fracture is in any of the other fingers, such as the middle finger or index finger, or the ring finger or little finger, then you should bandage it with the same finger that it is beside, or bandage them all continuously, which is better; or place a small metal plate on it, as we have said for the big toe.
And consider, in your recovery, the location, placement, and nature of the abscess or tumor, with the surgical procedure, with what is necessary when any of those occurs, as described in the narrative.
Chapter 14.
Of the healing of a fracture in the thigh bone.
A fractured thigh bone is often manifested by sensation, according to what is felt in the affected area above and below.
And the healing of it is done in such a way that the ligament is strengthened over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, and the patient lies on their stomach and face.
And then the assistant should stretch the entire ligament to its full length according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is close to the head of the thigh bone, then the ligament should be attached to the neck at the end of the femur, so that the extension occurs above it and the other ligament below the fracture.
Similarly, if the fracture is close to the head, then the ligament should be attached to it and the extension should occur below.
Then the doctor should bind the bone with both of his hands until it returns to its natural shape and the bone is rejoined by a good connection. After that, it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, unless an abscess occurs in the affected limb. But if an abscess occurs in it, then you should leave it alone for several days until the abscess has subsided and then return to your treatment.
The bandage should be made in the following way: it is necessary to wrap a hard bandage around the fracture, folding it two or three times, and remove any excess material from it. Then, wrap the thigh, extending it to reach the knee and the head of the thigh bone.
And insert a long ligament between the thigh and leg near the head, with its ends extending from both sides of the two parts, then wrap it around the thigh and leg so that any excess material from the bandage is covered.
Then place splints on the thigh at the site of the fracture and place one splint on the bone of the leg.
Then fill the space between the leg and thigh with soft cloths to reinforce the binding.
Then start the binding from the middle of the fracture with three or four strong layers and as you move away from the ligament at the site of the fracture, make the binding lighter, softer and less tight.
Then stretch both ends of the ligament down to the heel and strengthen both ends of the rods on the other side to prevent the ligament from slipping out of place.
Then leave the binding like that, so that no corrosion, abscess or swelling occurs in the affected limb, and if any of those occur, then be careful to loosen the binding and correct anything that has happened as we have often said before.
But if pus is present in the bone and cannot be removed, then it is necessary to bind the bone as well as possible; or cut into it and remove the pus, leaving the wound open to heal as described previously.
Sometimes, this thigh bone fracture is strengthened with rods that are adjusted to it, just as we have said in the case of the arm or forearm.
However, when healing is done in this way, the patient should not develop a limp; but if it heals on its own without the use of the other leg, then it is necessary for that patient to limp permanently.
And know that the thigh bone heals in 40 days or a little more or less, and all of this depends on the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter 15.
Of the healing of a fracture in the head of the thigh bone (femoral head).
Know that a fracture of the head of the thigh bone takes a long time to heal, but it often causes severe pain.
But if it is a fracture, then it is not a complete break but rather a crack or split in its parts, with or without a wound, and everything depends on the sensation.
And the healing of it is done by binding what has been broken in its parts with the mentioned methods until they are joined and healed as much as possible through equality, ease and construction.
Then apply a plaster and place a round rod on it if necessary, and strengthen above with appropriate binding.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have recommended in other fractures, such as abscess or similar symptoms and prevent any accidents that may occur during the healing process until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
Of the healing of a fracture in the leg (lower leg).
Know that in the leg there are two bones, one is large and called the shin bone (tibia), and the other is small and called the calf bone (fibula).
