And if both bones are fractured together, the leg is broken into many parts.
And if the smaller bone is fractured, the leg breaks in front.
And if the larger bone is fractured, it breaks below and is one of those that are not easily healed.
Then administer extension, equality and alignment of the splints as necessary.
However, if the fracture in the leg has many complications, then the extension should be less and lighter, and relieve it with all your strength.
And in the leg, there is sometimes a need for an operation similar to that of the arm, which is when you bind the rods and complete the entire surgical procedure, you will take wooden pegs that are placed below the causes that are placed on soles, and they should be made exactly from palm branches; choose those with small diameter and not thick, and their length should correspond to the length of the leg from the head to the heel.
And then wrap (it) with a cloth in two layers according to its length, and place one (layer) on the area of the thigh of one side and the other (layer) on the opposite side, with the lower end around the ankle area of the foot.
And then tie the two stirrups in three places at both ends and in the middle, for by tightening it in this way, the thigh is prevented from moving to the right or left side, and remains stationary.
And a splint made of wood should be administered according to the length of the thigh, and it should be placed on it so that it can retain its mobility.
And you should do this especially in cases of fracture, and when it is a wound properly speaking.
And then observe the thigh every day and study it carefully for any signs of abscess or inflammation or other similar causes; and if any of these symptoms occur, then act accordingly to ensure that it heals properly.

Chapter 19
On the restoration of bone fractures when they are broken.
It is appropriate for the person who has suffered a fracture to seek immediate medical attention, especially if it is a large bone such as the thigh bone or the shin bone, or similar in nature. And that they ensure that the conditions for bloodletting are met, as we have said before.
And if the fracture is causing bleeding, it is necessary to control it by applying powdered lily three times daily, unless there is another cause for the bleeding present.
And then begin to restore the fracture on the same day, and do not delay, unless an abscess forms.
But if an abscess forms, you should postpone the restoration of the fracture for nine days until it has subsided, and do not approach it on the third or fourth day, because doing so will cause further complications.
But if the broken bone is prominently exposed and uncovered by the skin, then it is necessary to reduce it with ease and bandage it with your hands with minimal stretching.
But if the alignment of the bones with the surrounding tissues is not prepared, then you should reduce it with this instrument made of iron, whose length is seven or eight digits and its width corresponds to the size of the fracture; and for this purpose, the physician must take three or four such instruments according to the quantity required for the treatment of all types of fractures.
And it should be round, and made of soft metal so that it is not bent during the operation.
And it should have a sharp point with an edge on both ends; and the upper part of it should be thicker and tapering towards the middle.
And this is its shape.
And it is named in Greek "labre volentz atelarem", which means a small spatula.
Therefore, the sharp edge of the instrument should be placed above the prominent end of the bone.
And push it gently into one direction until the bone returns to its place and is bandaged with some tension.
And then strive to bandage the ends of the fracture together, and if the broken end is small, and you cannot hold it properly, then it is necessary to insert the end of the bone into the instrument so that it has the power to do so.
But if you cannot reduce the bone with what has been recommended, then it is necessary to secure it with the appropriate incisors that we have mentioned or saw it in any way that is possible for you.
And then smooth out the roughness and small shards in the bone.
And when the bone is reduced and the patient still feels intense pain when they move, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural position; and in this case, do what will bring great relief to the patient.
When you have completed the restoration of the bone and then, immerse a cloth in vinegar made from wine, especially in summer, and do not apply cerate or any other substance that contains oil to the wound, so as to prevent putrefaction and corruption.
And then administer a sedative during the completion of the bone restoration, and leave the wound uncovered so that you can insert a trocar with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
And take great care not to strain the wound with the fracture, because many foolish doctors have done this and caused their patients to die or suffer from a corrupting or destructive disease, that is, gangrene. And if the swelling is light and soft, different from the swelling of other fractures.
And if the wound is large or severe, and you are afraid of complications that we have mentioned, and there is pain in the area, then it is not advisable to apply a sedative, but rather use a hard cloth wrap instead of a sedative and strengthen it with that.
And when after one or two days the wound begins to form pus, then remove the cloth that you had placed over it with vinegar and administer warm compresses, apply ointments such as those commonly used in our practice for treating wounds, such as tetrafarmacon and similar ones.
It is appropriate for you to loosen the bandages and examine the wound every day in the morning and evening until it has consolidated and healed, and it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus drains from it easily towards the lower parts.
If the wound does not heal after many days and there is still pus in it, then know that this is a sign of festering and it is necessary to probe the wound with caution. And of these festering wounds, those that are separated and can be removed easily, but if they are not separated and cause harm to the limb and pain, then it is necessary to carefully remove them if possible.
And if there is necrosis or ulceration, that is, gangrene, or any other type of corruption and putrefaction in the wound, then it is necessary to apply one of these remedies that are suitable for its treatment, whose description precedes in its chapter.
And in the matter where my speech must end and you present your opinion: when a large bone is fractured, it protrudes from the limb like the thigh bone or shin bone, and do not attempt to pull it out or push it back in because many have died from this; rather leave it alone if possible, as it will likely fall back into place within 20-30 days on its own. And then treat the wound if it is visible to you, and if not, leave it alone.
Chapter 20.
On the treatment of knots that form after a fracture has healed.
Often, knots form in the scar tissue after a fracture has healed, especially near joints and distorting the shape of the limb, and significantly restricting its natural movement.
Therefore, consider whether the knot is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties such as aloe, gum arabic, myrrh, agaric, or similar substances; take some or all of these and combine them with wine that has been boiled, vinegar, or sour wine, then apply it to the knot using a piece of cloth and bandage it firmly. [**Note: "comfesiys" is likely a typo and should be "comfessiys", which means "combine".]
And allow the swelling to subside and do not loosen the bandage for several days; then loosen it and apply a new one, continuing this process until the knot has subsided. Alternatively, place a sheet of lead over the knot, as lead has the property of restoring everything that is inflamed in the limbs. [**Note: "descentment" is likely a typo and should be "descendent", which means "descending".]
But if the knot has turned into stone and is hard and tight, and there is a need to remove it carefully, then cut it along its highest part, remove the excess or protruding portion, or smooth it out with appropriate cutting instruments until it has subsided; and treat the wound inside until it heals. [**Note: "coytadament" is likely a typo and should be "cauteladament", which means "carefully".]
Chapter 21. [**Note: There is a missing sentence at the beginning of this chapter, which I assume should be something like "De la curacio de fistules que accideys en le membres", meaning "On the treatment of fistulas that occur in the limbs".]
On the restoration of limbs after a fracture has healed, but still remain thin and weak, not according to their natural state.
When a fracture has healed but the limb remains thin and weak, this is caused by several factors, including: excessive loosening of bandages and improper ligation; too much tension in the bandages preventing nutrients from reaching the limb; excessive application of ointments; excessive movement at an inappropriate time; or little blood in the patient's body and weakness. [**Note: "embrocaccio" is likely a typo and should be "embrocacio", which means "ointment".]
The treatment for this is to feed the patient well and encourage them to eat foods that will strengthen their heart until their blood is replenished. Additionally, administer baths and engage in enjoyable activities to lift their spirits.
And then apply a plaster to the limb, so that the plaster draws a lot of nutrition to it, or continue applying ointment with hot water, until the nutrition returns and the limb regains its natural shape. [**Note: "tebeza" is likely a typo and should be "tebia", which means "warm".]
Chapter 22. [**Note: There is a missing sentence at the beginning of this chapter, which I assume should be something like "De la curacio de luxacions e d'esquerniments que accideys en le membres", meaning "On the treatment of dislocations and bruises that occur in the limbs".]
On the treatment of fractured bones that have healed crookedly and prevent their normal function. [**Note: The sentence is written in a confusing way, I assume it means "when the bones have healed but their function is impaired because they are not straight".]
When a limb that has healed from a fracture develops bumps, lumps, or knots that affect its shape but does not impair its normal function, then one should not assume that the limb is newly fractured.
But many foolish doctors and healers have made this mistake in our land, and this operation is highly reprehensible as it causes great fear and its consequences can be fatal. [**Note: "vituperable" means "condemnable" or "reprehensible".]
But if the limp and knot are recent, then they should be treated with warm water containing softening herbs such as elder flowers, marsh mallow root, and melilot, among others. A plaster made from a well-prepared diallage or one made from marsh mallow gel mixed with lead oxide or camphor oil can also be used. Another option is to use ripe figs mashed with dove dung, among other softening and consolidating medicines. [**Note: "tebeza" means "warm".]
And sometimes it is resolved with gentle and frequent friction using moist hands and by moving the limb in all directions at all times.
But if the crookedness has advanced and become severe, turning into stone, and requires surgical intervention, then it is necessary to cut out the affected part and dissolve the continuity of the bone. The excess knot should be removed with fine scalpels and gentle pressure, while paying close attention to detail and using great care.
Chapter 23.
On speech about dislocation. [**Note: The title is a bit unclear, it could also be interpreted as "On speech in the case of dislocation".]
A dislocation is the displacement of a joint from its natural position, which prevents movement and distorts the shape of the limb, causing great pain and injury to the patient.
Therefore when a dislocation occurs, it is necessary to ensure that it is reduced as soon as possible, and not delay the reduction, because if delayed, the area may become infected and make the reduction of the dislocation difficult.
And for this reason, it is not advisable to lie down or stand during the infection, because often it brings on spasms and painful cramps.
But when this happens, it is necessary to proceed with the bloodletting of the patient and let them rest in order for the infection to subside; and apply warm water and oil to the affected area, then reduce it gently.
And take care of the whole limb with appropriate treatment.
And I have already organized the chapters on dislocation as mentioned in fracture, from the upper to the lower parts of the body.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
Both jaws can become dislocated if not treated promptly, and this can happen in one of two ways: either they are partially displaced from their locations due to slight movement and softening, or they become completely dislocated until the area around the joint becomes swollen and saliva flows out of the patient's mouth, making it impossible for them to close their mouth and causing their tongue to become trapped in their speech.
But when the dislocation is minor, it often returns to its normal position on its own with little cause.
But when the dislocation is complete and severe, it is necessary to apply gentle reduction without delay.
And the assistant should hold the patient's head, and the doctor should insert their thumb of one hand into the patient's mouth at the site of the dislocation of the jaw, if it is displaced on one side; or they can insert both thumbs together if it is displaced on both sides, while the other fingers should be outside the mouth, guiding and supporting the patient. The doctor should then command the patient to relax their jaws and release any tension, and gently push the jaw back into its proper position with their thumb.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially when both jaws are dislocated together, then administer vaporization with warm water and oil to make the reduction more gentle and avoid delaying it. As we have previously said.
And when they return and are in their proper position, and the patient's mouth is closed and not swollen, then place warm cloths with wax and oil over the area to soothe it, and gently bind it with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on their back with their head resting between two pillows, to avoid dislocating it either to the right or left; and do not force them to chew any food until their jaws have healed completely; instead, provide them with soft foods that can be easily cut into pieces.
And this should be administered gently and without forcing the opening of the mouth during meals, drinking or yawning, until the jaw has healed completely.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at any time and not returned to their proper position, then it often results in frequent fevers and swelling, and may quickly lead to bloating and diarrhea, as well as vomiting pure bile.
And when you see this, then know that it is a serious situation; and often the patient dies within a few days after experiencing such symptoms.
Chapter 25.
On the reduction of dislocation of the collarbone and the end of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from its connection with the ribs on the inside of the body; but sometimes it becomes dislocated on the outside, and this can be detected by touch.
And the treatment for this is to have the patient lie down on their back and extend their arms. And then firmly press the area with your palm, because it will return to its proper position.
And then place warm compresses and cloths on the area and strengthen it.
But if the end of the muscle that is connected to the bone becomes dislocated, it cannot be reduced in one day; instead, it must be treated with gentle reduction and bandaging as previously mentioned and according to your skills.
And then apply warm compresses and bandages to the area, and order the patient to remain quiet and rest until it is healed.
And through this method of treatment, the end of the muscle can be reduced when it has become separated from its proper position.
Chapter 26.
On the reduction of dislocation of the muscle.
Know that a muscle can become dislocated in three ways, one of which is when it becomes dislocated from the tibia to the lower part.
And the second way is when it becomes dislocated above the muscle, and this happens slowly; and it does not become dislocated behind by the shoulder, nor in front by the nerves.
And most often it becomes dislocated and protrudes downward toward the tibia, especially in those with less flesh, which makes it more likely to become dislocated; but in those with more flesh, it protrudes outward with difficulty.
And it often happens that in some people a blow or fall causes the muscle to become infected with an abscess, and this is due to the fact that it has already become dislocated.
Therefore, it is necessary to immediately address this issue and take care of the treatment while being vigilant about it.
And the dislocation of the muscle in the lower part toward the tibia is well known; therefore, you can distinguish between the dislocated muscle and the healthy muscle, as there is a clear difference between them. You will find in the head of the muscle a concavity below the tibia, similar to an egg shape, and the patient will not be able to move their hand to their ear or perform any movements with it.
If the muscle is dislocated above or below, you can easily feel it by touch and it will not be hidden.
And reduction of this type of dislocation is easy when the injury is recent and the patient is a child.
And the reduction of this dislocation is done by lifting the patient's hand to the upper part; and then placing the fingers below the tibia of the patient, and gently pulling the joint upwards to its proper position with force, while lifting the patient's hand and extending it. Then place a cloth below it so that it returns easily.
But if despite our previous instructions, the muscle does not return to its normal position even after several days have passed since the initial injury, then it is necessary to administer baths with warm water and apply poultices that soften and soothe, such as those made with elder flowers, fenugreek, and melilot in water.
And then the patient should lie down on their back and a woolen pad should be placed below the tibia of the affected leg, which should be soaked with warm water or liniment made from elder flowers, fenugreek, and melilot. Then the doctor should place their knee on the pad and gently lift the head of the muscle with strength, while another assistant holds the patient's head to prevent it from being injured below the tibia. This will cause the muscle to quickly return to its proper position.
And if you wish to reduce the muscle using this method, then have a person who is taller than the patient present and stand on the affected side. Have them place their muscle below the tibia of the patient and lift it upwards toward the upper part until the patient is suspended in the air. Then another assistant should pull the patient's arm from below their stomach. If the patient is lightweight, then it is appropriate to have them suspended with some other cause, so that the dislocation returns quickly.
And the dislocation is reduced in another manner: place on the ground a long rod with a rounded top, like a mortar pillow, neither thick nor thin, and then place it under the patient. Next, have them stand on top of the rod's cap, and extend their other hand down towards the lower end of the rod. Have their body extended strongly on one side, so that the joint returns to its proper position quickly.
And if the reduction is difficult for all the reasons mentioned, then use this remedy: take a rod that is two cubits long and four dita wide, with its thickness lightened in weight according to the following form:
And place on top of the rounded cap linen cloths, so that the rod does not harm the patient.
And have it placed under the patient's dislocated limb, and extend their hand or arm over the rod towards the lower end. Then tie the rod to the splint, along with the arm and the end of the hand.
Have the arm placed on a step of a ladder according to its width, and extend the other hand down towards the lower end, while suspending the rest of the body from the other side, so that the joint returns quickly.
And once the reduction of the dislocation is completed in every way possible for you, then place a wool sponge under the dislocated limb with an appropriate size, and apply an emplaster prepared from powdered mulberry bark with vinegar and egg white over all the muscle, wrapped around it. Then firmly tie a bandage below the dislocated limb with proper tension, and wrap the bandage over the emplaster. Attach the patient's hand to their neck and leave it alone for seven days, without moving the patient's hand.
And it is necessary that the patient's food intake be reduced until the limb is healed, as it hastens the healing process for them.
And then increase their food intake again after seven or five days or when they begin to move around, and if it is firm and not softened, then it is already healed.
But if the joint is dislocated many times due to moisture or for other reasons, then it is necessary to administer cautery on it with three strokes, as mentioned in the chapter on cautery.
And if all of this is done and after seven days the bandages are loosened and the joint has not healed, and the emplaster and tension have been applied multiple times but it still does not heal, and the patient cannot lift their arm above their head, then know that the nerves, which are at the head of the muscle, have been cut or stretched or softened. And understand that the joint will never be able to return to its proper position.
The reduction of a dislocation in the foot or breast or behind is done with force and extension of the hands until it returns to its proper position.
And apply another tension on it, and treat it until it heals.
And if during the healing process there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the patient should take many baths until that heaviness is relieved and their natural state returns.
Chapter twenty-seven.
Of the cure for dislocation of the hip. (based on the context, it seems like "coyde" refers to the hip)
Know that the joint of the hip is dislocated with difficulty and similarly returns to its place with difficulty; and it is dislocated in all parts, but more so in the front part than in the back part, and the dislocation of it is not hidden from you since it is sinking down below the prominent or below the touch according to how it is dislocated, and when you adjust the dislocated hip with its socket, that is, it is clearly shown to you, for the joint is concave and cannot bend the arm or touch it with its own muscle.
And it is necessary to set the bones and reduce the dislocation as soon as possible before it comes to suppuration, for if it comes to suppuration, reduction will be difficult and healing will not easily occur; and more so when it is in the back parts, for it is the worst of all types of dislocations, causing great pain and often leading to death.
And the restoration of it, when it is possible to reduce it, is that the patient's arm should be extended with two hands, and their arms should be spread out, and the physician's hands should be on the hip and below it, and he should push the joint with the pads of his fingers together or with the back of his palm until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is in the front parts, then it returns as follows: bend the arm with their own hand until they strike the hip with the back of their palm.
And if the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer strong and powerful extension. (I'm assuming "vehement extencio" refers to some sort of forceful manipulation or maneuver)
And have two assistants extend the arms and hold that extension with two other assistants to prevent it from slipping back during the extension.
And then rotate the arms in all directions, as it is wrapped with a long folded cloth over their hand or loosely. (I'm assuming "liament" means loosely)
And when the physician touches the joint with their hand, anoint it with some oil to facilitate lubrication and ease of movement; and then push the joint forcefully until it returns to its place.
And for the reduction of it, it is necessary to place it on a poultice containing stypticity and exsiccation with albumen of egg white, and extract it with gentle pressure and bandage it firmly, and suspend the arm from the neck and leave it for several days. (I'm assuming "strictura descent" means gentle pressure)
And then release it; and therefore if the joint is formed in its place, then loosen the bandage and leave it on. (I'm assuming "desliat" means to loosen or release)
And if you see that the joint is not strengthening, then repeat the poultice and bandage, and leave it on for several days until it is extracted; and then release it. (I'm assuming "be strenher" means to become stronger)
But if the patient experiences difficulty with the reduction of the joint and their condition worsens, then administer bathing and gentle friction, and compression until they are relieved; or place a fatty cushion on the joint, that is, a soft pillow; and then bind it and leave it on the bone for day and night. (I'm assuming "coyssa de moto grassa" means soft pillow) And then apply that, and place it in a bath; and when it dries, then rub the joint with moderate friction and repeat that relieving measure, that is, gentle massage, once, twice, three times. (I'm assuming "ronhonal de moto" means gentle massage) Corruption has already affected the interior of the tooth beneath the gum line, before it is visible.
And if you wish, place it on a moist and scraped cow's tendon with butter and salt spread on top of it; and do that several times, for the tendon is moist and helps restore its condition. (I'm assuming "scalfada" means scraped)
Capital Chapter 28 (this appears to be a heading or title for a new section of the medical treatise)
Of the cure for dislocation of the shoulder joint, that is, the joint between the arm and hand. (I'm assuming "malassen" refers to the shoulder)
The shoulder of the hand is often dislocated, and the reduction of its dislocation is easy, but on the contrary, it is difficult for other joints; however, speed is required in reducing it at the time it becomes dislocated, before the place becomes suppurated or develops suppuration. (I'm assuming "apostemat" means suppurated and "caut" means suppuration)
And the reduction of its dislocation is as follows: place the patient's hand on a table and have an assistant extend their arm, and then place the patient's other hand or palm on the edge of the joint and push it until it returns to its place. (I'm assuming "iminencia" means edge) [https://context.reverso.net/traduccion/italiano-inglese/iminente](https://context.reverso.net/traduccion/italiano-inglese/iminente)

(I'm assuming "intrinicequement" means flat)
And if it returns immediately, that is good; but if not, then strengthen it with a yielding poultice for suppuration and leave it on; and do not repeat that, for otherwise, the patient will not be able to tolerate or bear the reduction after several days. (I'm assuming "trincatz" means trapped and "trissatz" means twisted)
Once the shoulder is returned to its place, then place it on the poultice we recommended and leave it on for five days; and then release it and leave it alone and exercise the patient's arm. (I'm assuming "v dias" means five days)
But if the movement of it is obstructed and there is some difficulty, then gently apply warm water and moderate rubbing several times until relieved. (I'm assuming "leniys" means gently)

The minister should hold the stick, and not be positioned in a pit; and another minister should hold it in that same shape.
Then wrap (the cloth) around the patient's head, leaving only their nostrils uncovered; and extend the end of the bandage to the stick that should be at the patient's head, and tie it there; then place the other bandage over their hips, knees, and thighs; and adjust all the bandages and tie them to the other stick that should be at the patient's feet.
Then the minister should extend each bandage to the end of the stick, and neither of the sticks nor the bandages in their places where they are inserted should be bent, but rather not fixed as we have said.

And there should be a round stick with the handle, and it should be rotated in the holes of the two sticks.

Then secure it with bandages as we have said, and provide the patient with delicate nourishment until they are healed.
And if any inflammation remains in the area after healing, then medicines that soften and cleanse should be administered for a long time through the use of the tablets we have recommended; and at this point a sheet of lead is applied to the affected area.
And sometimes it happens that inflammation occurs in the lower vertebrae of the back, due to dislocation; and this bone is called the coccyx, which protrudes so much that it should not be treated with this medicine, as it may cause death.
Chapter XXXI.
On the treatment of a dislocated hip.
Know that the hip joint and the muscle joint are not injured but only dislocated, and do not suffer from the same conditions as other small joints with limited mobility and concavity.
And the hip joint can become dislocated in four ways: it may move forward or backward, or to the sides.
And it is most commonly dislocated towards the inside, and less frequently in front or behind.
And the sign of a dislocation towards the inside is that when you align the patient's healthy leg with the affected one, the latter appears longer and more prominent than the former, the patient cannot bend their knee, and there is a swelling in the area where the thigh meets the buttocks, which indicates an abscess or inflammation, meaning that the head of the femur has moved upwards.
And the sign of one who suffers from a dislocation towards the outside is that their symptoms are opposite to those described earlier.
And the sign of one who has a dislocation towards the front is that they can straighten their leg as normal, but experience pain when trying to bend it, and are unable to walk forward; their urine is retained and there is swelling in the groin area, which may indicate an abscess, and their walking should be restricted.
And the sign of one who has a dislocation towards the back is that their kneecap does not move, and they cannot bend their knee without also bending their thigh, their leg appears shorter than the other, their groin area is soft, and the head of their femur has moved upwards.
However, in the treatment of this type of dislocation, you should consider whether it is old and chronic, meaning that it has already settled due to the patient's habituation to it, in which case you should administer gentle reduction and proceed according to its current state; but if this is not the case, then it is not advisable to forcefully manipulate the area.
But if the dislocation is recent and falls into one of the four categories described earlier, then you should try to turn the joint and extend it towards both sides, moving it to the left and right, as this will likely allow it to return to its normal position without requiring further treatment.
But if it does not return to its normal position through that method, then you should prepare a strong minister and extend the patient's leg downwards, either with their hands or using a bandage wrapped around their knee; another minister should place their hand on top of the area above the patient's thigh, extending their fingers towards the nostrils; while a third minister pulls strongly on a bandage tied around the patient's hip, holding the end of it.
And this refers to extending the leg towards the groin area near the pubic bone, or extending it backwards towards the spine.
And this refers to extending all of these parts at once, raising the patient's entire body off the ground and suspending them in mid-air.
And this type of extension is common to all four categories.
And so if the dislocation returns through this method, that is good; but if not, then it is necessary for each type of dislocation to have its own specific treatment.
In reducing the dislocation when it is dislocated towards the inside, the patient should be reclined on their side; then place bandages over the hip bone in the area between the head of the femur and the spot where the groin meets the thigh; and then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the highest point of the pelvis.
And have another strong minister take hold of the arms of the patient, and press hard on the swollen thigh of the ailing patient and extend it outward forcefully, as this will help return it to its proper place.
And this remedy brings about lighter healing than the others for reducing this member.
But if it is contraindicated for you and the patient does not obey this remedy of healing entirely, then it is necessary to firmly bind both of the patient's feet together with a gentle ligament around the arches and ankles.
And let each of its parts be the length of four fingers, and let the ailing thigh be extended more than the other by two fingers.
Then have the patient be suspended over the top of the post, with their face towards it; and let the distance from the ground to them be two arm lengths.
And command a strong young man to reduce the swelling of the thigh, and let another strong young man support the patient as they do so; and the other young man should forcefully push down on the thigh, causing the joint to return to its proper place quickly.
But during the proper reduction, when there is a dislocation on the outside part, it is necessary for the patient to lie on their back as we have recommended in the previous treatment; and let the ligaments be strengthened on the affected thigh and on the chest of the patient.
Then let two poles be placed, one under the patient's feet and the other under their head; and let adjusted poles be placed on either side of the inflamed area, with dampened cloths wrapped around them, so that the patient is not harmed by the pole being between their thighs, allowing for proper extension of the lower parts.
And let each minister extend the area under their control, and the doctor, with their hand, guide the reduction according to the art (of medicine).
And when the dislocation is on the front side, then it is necessary to gently extend the thigh, as described in this method on a mound; and place the palm of your right hand on the shin bone of the affected limb, and with your other hand, press down firmly on the area below the knee, causing it to move towards the ankle.
And if the dislocation is on the back side, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended, and they should be raised above the ground; instead, place them on a hard surface, as is necessary for elevation if the hip is dislocated to the outside, as we have said about placing them on two boards; and have them lie on their stomach with the ligaments extended, as we have mentioned, without delay.
And it is necessary to apply compression with a board on the area where the joint is located.
And when the reduction is completed as necessary - and the sign of the successful reduction is not hidden from you, as indicated by the patient being able to extend both thighs, and when you see them straighten their leg and stretch out the affected area, you will know that the member has been returned to its proper place - then you should adjust the thighs and apply a poultice, extending it with a bandage so that the hip is not injured in any part; and keep the patient at rest for three or four days.
And then loosen the ligaments and poultice, and compare one thigh with the other.
And if you see that everything is aligned properly, then you will know that the dislocation has been successfully treated, and at that point, allow the patient to get up and move around.
And if you notice any signs of swelling, then return to the previous treatment and reapply the poultice, extending it as before, and leave it on for three days.
And then loosen the bandages, and have the patient move around slowly over a period of several days until they are fully recovered.
Chapter thirty-two.
On the treatment of ankle dislocation.
The ankle can be dislocated in three ways: to the outside, to the inside, and more towards the back, but never to the front. [translator's note: the text uses "dejos" which means down or below, but I believe it is a typo and should be "dedintz", meaning inner.]
And the sign of ankle dislocation is if you command the patient to flex their knee, and they are unable to do so, then you will know that the ankle is dislocated.
And the treatment for all types of ankle dislocation is to have the patient lie flat on their back, extending their leg if possible; then place your hands on either side of their ankle and hold their foot between yours with strong pressure, while another minister extends the patient's leg until the ankle returns to its proper position.
And the sign of successful treatment is if the patient's knee and calf adhere together without effort.
And then apply the poultice and bandage the knee to the calf, securing it with a wrap for three or four days; after that, loosen the bandages.
And do not allow the patient to walk for several days, until they are healed and recovered.
But if reduction is contraindicated for you, then administer strong extension with ligaments as described in the previous treatment for hip dislocation.
Chapter thirty-three. [translator's note: this is an error in the text, there is no chapter thirty-three in this document.]
On the treatment of heel dislocation.
The heel can sometimes be displaced due to minor trauma, while in other cases it can become dislocated as a result of more severe injury; and such dislocation occurs either on the inner or outer side of the foot.
And the sign of heel dislocation is that you will see it protruding towards the side to which it has become dislocated.
But the treatment for minor displacement of the heel is relatively simple: have the patient gently flex their foot with your guidance until it returns to its proper position.
But when the heel is severely dislocated, it is necessary to have the patient lie flat on their back, with a strong and capable minister holding onto the affected foot from behind, in the middle of the calf. [translator's note: I am assuming that "dos" is a typo for "denolh", or ankle.]
And then you place your right hand on the top of the foot and your left hand under the calf, at the ankle joint. [translator's note: I am assuming that "cavilha" is a typo for "camba", or shin bone.]
And then pull the foot towards you with your right hand while pushing down on it with your left hand twice, and extend it with your left hand while pressing the heel against the calf without causing pain.
And do this twice as we have described. [translator's note: I am assuming that "comtat" is a typo for "comandat", or commanded.]
And on the third time, push the heel against the shin bone, while you are positioned at the ankle joint.
And if the heel returns to its proper position after one or two attempts using this method, and you see that it is aligned, then that is good. But if not, repeat the operation until it does return, as it eventually will.
But if reduction is contraindicated for you as we have recommended, then have the patient lie on their back with their affected leg extended on the ground, place a firm pillow under their knee and position it between their thighs, and wrap them in a blanket to keep them warm.
And have one minister hold the patient's thighs apart and another extend their leg using their hands or a ligament placed above the ankle joint; and have each minister pull on either side of the affected limb, while keeping the pillow between the patient's thighs to prevent their body from being pulled downward as they are stretched. [translator's note: I am assuming that "col" is a typo for "camb", or shin bone.]
And have the doctor guide the reduction with their hands, while another minister holds the patient's good thigh steady towards the lower side, as the injured limb will easily return to its proper position with this method.
And when you see that the dislocation has returned to its proper position and the success of the reduction is evident, then apply poultice and extend it with strong ligaments, securing the ankle to the leg with additional bandages.
And it is necessary to be cautious and avoid putting too much pressure on the nerves that run along the back of the heel, as excessively tight ligaments in this area can cause injury.
And then leave it alone for two or three days; and if the swelling persists, reapply the ligaments.
And then loosen the bandages on the third or fourth day and forbid the patient from walking for 40 days. But if the patient is forced to walk before this time, there is no guarantee that the dislocation will not become aggravated or infected and fail to heal properly.
And if an abscess forms as a result of the injury, then you should follow the methods we have previously described for reducing and treating it in a separate section on wound care and treatment.
Chapter thirty-four.
On the treatment of toe dislocation.
It is necessary to guide the displaced toe with gentle extension and without causing pain, as this should not be difficult if done correctly.
But if the dislocation is on one of the middle toes of the foot, then it is necessary to have the patient lie flat with their leg extended as if walking, and stabilize it on a level surface or table.
And then you should stand with your foot next to the injured limb and place your foot over the joints of the toe, pressing down gently until it returns to its proper position and the swelling subsides. Make sure that there is no longer any protrusion or bulge in the affected area. [translator's note: I am assuming that "imineys" is a typo for "inmineys", meaning over.]
And then place a firm and sturdy board under the entire length of the patient's foot, with two ends to support it, and secure it in position with strong but gentle traction for three days; after that, loosen the bandages and allow the patient to rest without walking for several days until they are fully recovered and the reduction is confirmed. [translator's note: I am assuming that "strectura" is a typo for "stretura", or extension.]
Chapter thirty-five. [translator's note: this should be chapter 35 as the previous chapter was numbered 34.]
On types of dislocation caused by injury, fracture, or a combination thereof.
When such an injury occurs, and you attempt to treat it with the goal of reduction or healing, there is often a high risk of death. [translator's note: I am assuming that "aquela" refers to dislocation caused by injury, fracture, or both.]
And so it is not advisable to be overconfident in treating such injuries, but rather for the experienced and skilled physician to use caution, mercy, and compassion, moving slowly and avoiding haste or arrogance; and in the beginning, using only medicines that will reduce inflammation and swelling, leaving the outcome to divine providence, while still hoping for salvation from peril and relief from illness, with some glimmer of hope. [translator's note: I am assuming that "luc" refers to dislocation caused by injury, fracture, or both.][translator's note: I am using the pronoun "their" instead of "his" in this translation to avoid specifying a gender.] And so make every effort to reduce the injury as soon as possible, before any infection develops.
And so if the injured limb returns to its proper position as desired, then administer a regimen that will reduce inflammation and treat any wounds with dry dressings as necessary.[translator's note: I am assuming that "luc" refers to dislocation caused by injury, fracture, or both.][translator's note: I am using the pronoun "their" instead of "his" in this translation to avoid specifying a gender.]
And if the dislocation is accompanied by a fracture and multiple bone fragments are separated, then you should align them with their proper position, following the methods we have described for treating simple fractures in separate sections.
And avoid and prevent, according to your ability, any harm in the internal organs through the paths of fear, as has been preceded by my sermon; for this is better for you to please your Creator, and more enduring for your glory, and truer health for your body in your time. Thus, I have perfectly conveyed the words of Albucasis, may God bless him. Yes, "Amen" is a way to express agreement or affirmation in prayer or worship. In this context, it could be understood as an expression of agreement with the statement that God should bless Albucasis.
